                    </a>

                  <span class="header_dropdown_menu__content"><p>Learn how Red Bull augmented Microsoft EOP to prevent attacks in <a href="https://perception-point.io/resources/case-studies/international-food-and-beverage-company-augments-microsoft-eop-to-prevent-attacks/">the case study, here</a>.</p>
</span>
                </div>

		<div  data-elementor-type="single" data-elementor-id="1899" class="elementor elementor-1899 elementor-location-single post-16908 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-attack-trends blog_author-igal-lytzki" data-elementor-post-type="elementor_library">
			<div  class="elementor-element elementor-element-435e2df e-flex e-con-boxed e-con e-parent" data-id="435e2df" data-element_type="container">
					<div  class="e-con-inner">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-23af678 elementor-widget elementor-widget-breadcrumbs" data-id="23af678" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="breadcrumbs.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<p id="breadcrumbs"><span><span><a href="https://perception-point.io/">Perception Point</a></span> » <span><a href="https://perception-point.io/blog/">Blog</a></span> » <span><a href="https://perception-point.io/blog/category/attack-trends/">Attack Trends</a></span> » <span class="breadcrumb_last" aria-current="page"><strong>Revenge RAT Malware is back: From Microsoft Excel macros to Remote Access Trojan</strong></span></span></p>				</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-c4c7848 elementor-widget elementor-widget-heading" data-id="c4c7848" data-element_type="widget" data-settings="{&quot;_animation&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;_animation_mobile&quot;:&quot;none&quot;}" data-widget_type="heading.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<p class="elementor-heading-title elementor-size-default">April 7, 2022</p>				</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-32b01ba elementor-widget elementor-widget-heading" data-id="32b01ba" data-element_type="widget" data-settings="{&quot;_animation&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;_animation_delay&quot;:200,&quot;_animation_mobile&quot;:&quot;none&quot;}" data-widget_type="heading.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<h1 class="elementor-heading-title elementor-size-default">Revenge RAT Malware is back: From Microsoft Excel macros to Remote Access Trojan</h1>				</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-554c7d4 elementor-widget__width-initial elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="554c7d4" data-element_type="widget" data-settings="{&quot;_animation&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;_animation_delay&quot;:400,&quot;_animation_mobile&quot;:&quot;none&quot;}" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
									In this blog post, we go through the attack chain of a recent Revenge RAT malware campaign. By going deeper and analyzing the attack chain, we can better understand the malware’s capabilities.								</div>
				</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-072e1f1 elementor-widget elementor-widget-post-info" data-id="072e1f1" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="post-info.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
							<ul class="elementor-inline-items elementor-icon-list-items elementor-post-info">
								<li class="elementor-icon-list-item elementor-repeater-item-844ed94 elementor-inline-item">
													<span class="elementor-icon-list-text elementor-post-info__item elementor-post-info__item--type-custom">
										Igal Lytzki, IR Team					</span>
								</li>
				</ul>
						</div>
				</div>
					</div>
				</div>
		<div  class="elementor-element elementor-element-c646816 e-flex e-con-boxed e-con e-parent" data-id="c646816" data-element_type="container">
					<div  class="e-con-inner">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-b27becd e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="b27becd" data-element_type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-e7094d2 post-content elementor-widget__width-initial elementor-widget-tablet__width-initial elementor-widget-mobile__width-inherit elementor-widget elementor-widget-theme-post-content" data-id="e7094d2" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="theme-post-content.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					
<p>In this blog post, we review one of the recent <a href="https://perception-point.io/guides/malware/10-malware-examples-and-6-world-famous-attacks/">malware examples</a> that has resurfaced.</p>



<p>First observed in 2016, <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/revenge">Revenge RAT</a> is a <a href="https://perception-point.io/guides/malware/malware-types-examples-how-modern-anti-malware-works/">malware </a>that is classified as a Remote Access Trojan. This means that threat actors usually use it to remotely control infected PCs, surveil users by capturing keystrokes, watching them via webcam, or even listening to their microphones.</p>



<p>Through Revenge RAT malware, threat actors can remotely execute malicious tasks on the user’s computer and steal any sensitive data available to them.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-the-revenge-rat-malware-phishing-email">The Revenge RAT Malware Phishing Email</h2>



<p>Like almost every other attack these days, Revenge RAT malware is delivered via <a href="https://perception-point.io/channel-coverage/email-security/">email</a>, the number one attack vector for <a href="https://perception-point.io/news/cyberattacks-are-becoming-more-sophisticated/">cyberattacks</a>.</p>



<p>The user receives an email that appears to be an urgent invoice request. The email&nbsp;instructs the user to open up the attached invoice, an Excel file, and enable both editing and content, in order&nbsp;to “view” the content of the invoice. In reality, enabling editing and content activates the attack chain flow, which we will explore further later on in this post.</p>



<p>It is important to note that there are some obvious warning signs within the email:&nbsp;</p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li>The email subject is the exact name of the attached file, which indicates a low effort attempt by the threat actor to manipulate the user or convince them that the invoice is real.&nbsp;</li>



<li>The email contains an explanation of how to open the document, which usually indicates&nbsp;that the threat actor is employing a <a href="https://us.norton.com/internetsecurity-emerging-threats-what-is-social-engineering.html">social engineering</a> technique by&nbsp;trying to get the user to perform the exact commands required to execute the <a href="https://perception-point.io/guides/malware/how-prevent-malware-attacks/">malware</a>.</li>
</ol>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/DBrZBafz2peTWVo0_QPUCp6BNtoXZi_ru--m0qmTWiyFMyRKDUJjnr-1S9v-_tPCRNIF6tZqU3UOgLCvjVIogX8OleFCncX66OSSk8FrAZgj0vGo2Uq9E5rZLrb5hUaqgx_7igu-"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/DBrZBafz2peTWVo0_QPUCp6BNtoXZi_ru--m0qmTWiyFMyRKDUJjnr-1S9v-_tPCRNIF6tZqU3UOgLCvjVIogX8OleFCncX66OSSk8FrAZgj0vGo2Uq9E5rZLrb5hUaqgx_7igu-" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 1: Revenge RAT malware phishing email</figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><a href="https://hubs.la/Q019Q93z0"><img fetchpriority="high" decoding="async" width="1024" height="535" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%201024%20535'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-18034" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-1024x535.png.webp 1024w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-300x157.png.webp 300w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-768x401.png.webp 768w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2.png.webp 1200w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-1024x535.png.webp" /><noscript><img fetchpriority="high" decoding="async" width="1024" height="535" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-1024x535.png.webp" alt="" class="wp-image-18034" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-1024x535.png.webp 1024w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-300x157.png.webp 300w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2-768x401.png.webp 768w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/PP-2204-T2G-Blog_Article-01-v2-2.png.webp 1200w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></noscript></a></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-the-revenge-rat-malware-attack-flow">The Revenge RAT Malware Attack Flow</h2>



<p>The components of the Revenge RAT malware attack occur throughout multiple stages. The image below shows the steps involved in the attack:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/vq0K3M7DRiQWhs-7sQgUqU_jKtM-XaF0Zjs2-736b11wtBNh59ogwZjCe_ONuZznynkEZWzbyHBOJaEZB5QN_BXBfMQGN5Vussnq0YoEMQOqqv_3-fYw7qqTbDJj6bfWEU6mFrB2"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/vq0K3M7DRiQWhs-7sQgUqU_jKtM-XaF0Zjs2-736b11wtBNh59ogwZjCe_ONuZznynkEZWzbyHBOJaEZB5QN_BXBfMQGN5Vussnq0YoEMQOqqv_3-fYw7qqTbDJj6bfWEU6mFrB2" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 2: Revenge RAT malware attack flow</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p><strong>Step 1: </strong>First, the XLS file (“QuickBooks Invoice Enclosed 10001144747631.xls”) is used in the attack vector by executing the macros it contains to run mshta.exe on a file located on the C2 server.</p>



<p><strong>Step 2:</strong> Then, “Enc.txt” (the executed file) executes another file on the C2 server using PowerShell.</p>



<p><strong>Step 3:</strong> Next, the executed file (“Server.txt”) creates a folder located on the user&#8217;s computer; inside, 4 files are created as the file starts execution of one of the its new files.</p>



<p><strong>Step 4:</strong> The first file executed (“IICOZODNLSRPOHUBAJWZFK.ps1”) creates a task that will run on the user’s computer every 3 minutes and will execute “IICOZODNLSRPOHUBAJWZFK.vbs”. This command contains code to execute “IICOZODNLSRPOHUBAJWZFK.hta” that will then execute “YHZYJGKGYAFXQEZFSRCPPY.ps1” (containing obfuscated Revenge RAT malware).</p>



<p><strong>Step 5:</strong> Upon the execution of “YHZYJGKGYAFXQEZFSRCPPY.ps1”, two files within the file are used to run the Revenge RAT malware.</p>



<p><strong>Step 6: </strong>The Revenge RAT malware communicates with the C2 server and the threat actor obtains a new C&amp;C computer for his collection.</p>



<p>Below we provide a detailed analysis of files and commands used during the various stages of the Revenge RAT malware attack.</p>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-initial-access">Initial Access</h2>



<p>The initial document, “QuickBooks Invoice Enclosed 10001144747631.xls”, contains <a href="https://support.microsoft.com/en-us/office/quick-start-create-a-macro-741130ca-080d-49f5-9471-1e5fb3d581a8#:~:text=If%20you%20have%20tasks%20in,your%20mouse%20clicks%20and%20keystrokes.">macros</a>. The creator of the document sets these commands to perform an action or a set of actions within the Excel document.</p>



<p>In the email, the threat actor explains that in order to view the content of the document the user must enable editing and enable content. Included in the document are two warning signs that there may be macros.</p>



<p>Opening up the document, the user sees a clean Excel sheet and must click on “Enable Editing” and “Enable Content.” Once clicked, the <a href="https://perception-point.io/blog/malicious-office-macros-detecting-similarity-in-the-wild-2/">malicious macro</a> code begins to work.</p>



<p>The macro code is obfuscated within other text, making it difficult for most users to understand:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/_WABDuKMtxrbNv7x5LWdhg4inYUhzERPLL9vdUID9SMOMFYgcxBeOA4LSyxjjpAKoYwwmyGmHHPeQ5eS4Ez2uQVcffvXBti-oMAUmTjfYcDi917wVk7ID2mQvNKJNadVhPznRu76"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/_WABDuKMtxrbNv7x5LWdhg4inYUhzERPLL9vdUID9SMOMFYgcxBeOA4LSyxjjpAKoYwwmyGmHHPeQ5eS4Ez2uQVcffvXBti-oMAUmTjfYcDi917wVk7ID2mQvNKJNadVhPznRu76" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 3: The obfuscated code</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>Three <a href="https://support.microsoft.com/en-us/office/find-or-replace-text-and-numbers-on-a-worksheet-0e304ca5-ecef-4808-b90f-fdb42f892e90#:~:text=Find%20%26%20Replace%20dialog-,To%20replace%20text%20or%20numbers%2C%20press%20Ctrl%2BH%2C%20or,Click%20Replace%20or%20Replace%20All.">replace functions</a> act to replace embedded strings within the “WHBKJDFVRUXXYRDLFLHVGG” variable. The program then runs the content of the variable by triggering the “Create” method under the Win32_Process class (WMI class represents a process on an operating system).</p>



<p>The de-obfuscated string looks like this:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/1aUMwrXkwtHUEtbyvt4O5WRVQSljbHQCnOsLJiuPUJag-5tSFOQFP9SQHZWLUqQFAyfplr76KHIs_WLzErJ3C_zF-iEo7GmSY8Ap8KGPJYWYV8ZiDiSWk82aC6RzPHXO4ysDW9Y1"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/1aUMwrXkwtHUEtbyvt4O5WRVQSljbHQCnOsLJiuPUJag-5tSFOQFP9SQHZWLUqQFAyfplr76KHIs_WLzErJ3C_zF-iEo7GmSY8Ap8KGPJYWYV8ZiDiSWk82aC6RzPHXO4ysDW9Y1" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 4: The de-obfuscated code</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>The macros execute “Mshta.exe”, which is a known signed Microsoft program. It can proxy execution of malware (execute HTML applications files) and establish a connection with the C2 server in order to download a file named “Enc.txt” (which is an additional payload for the attack chain).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/qNy4kDilZNrZRYUy_IxzIyoVIpqAzRwpBNFfRNWJJKxdhOStz2wmkr9HLqM6mMsFuP-ZZ7jVs3bP83kcf91antcIqv4j1Nf9Y2Ul2fmWpQzzicp0zT4X0B97Sl_u0E00bj15wglc"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/qNy4kDilZNrZRYUy_IxzIyoVIpqAzRwpBNFfRNWJJKxdhOStz2wmkr9HLqM6mMsFuP-ZZ7jVs3bP83kcf91antcIqv4j1Nf9Y2Ul2fmWpQzzicp0zT4X0B97Sl_u0E00bj15wglc" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 5: Mshta.exe</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/MsW9Bqi1iY9YnNG0KXlCxGlX9NWmbyijuETfKetfkHMHH9JePr8hChgYF9YiXWvolmUdO3j8hJIWCEEWiJz2joj7tCqcXGg76HXX7hnm4CYjDsNsKiEnG8VkIh5DoeE_5dLqbzYT"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/MsW9Bqi1iY9YnNG0KXlCxGlX9NWmbyijuETfKetfkHMHH9JePr8hChgYF9YiXWvolmUdO3j8hJIWCEEWiJz2joj7tCqcXGg76HXX7hnm4CYjDsNsKiEnG8VkIh5DoeE_5dLqbzYT" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 6: Enc.txt</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>Enc.txt is yet another obfuscated file; it contains a script tag which will be executed through mshta.exe:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/QJPGTMDMCzP8xP-c8Bgaa6lijoUQ-8Za0rR-m9-siY3rb8tBDeI4G7ru_TVH-rOlw4mnkBLsEvurAFik1IPmIAKlrq-7OnVZCTbH7P1w2_Mhc7p7ccRUgZOpBcbk-2zyluiRviPL"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/QJPGTMDMCzP8xP-c8Bgaa6lijoUQ-8Za0rR-m9-siY3rb8tBDeI4G7ru_TVH-rOlw4mnkBLsEvurAFik1IPmIAKlrq-7OnVZCTbH7P1w2_Mhc7p7ccRUgZOpBcbk-2zyluiRviPL" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 7: Script tag within Enc.txt</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>The script is written in Visual Basic, which we know because there is a &#8220;language&#8221; indicator in the opening tag. By the end of the script, it executes a <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/scripting/overview?view=powershell-7.2#:~:text=PowerShell%20is%20a%20cross%2Dplatform,Windows%2C%20Linux%2C%20and%20macOS.">PowerShell</a> command, which downloads another file from the C2 server and executes its content.</p>



<p>Below are the variables (and their values) of the PowerShell command:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>AHJTRRXVBYENECJTZIBQCT: StreamReader Class Object</li>



<li>ARYSWDUYBLHGDITSWDFEOG: WebRequest Class Object</li>



<li>TESIORILDCDTLCYOWDHSGO: Create, (invoked from WebRequest) creates web request to the other file located on the C2 server</li>



<li>KGHJKOKAPLPTBRQZIRBJZS: GetResponse</li>



<li>BKCOKSGBGDNXDEAXSBNVJU: GetResponseStream</li>



<li>ZUVEOIPLZEGADWFECARLXA: ReadToEnd</li>
</ul>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/Weff8wqn810hKciXuD4p8NmdoSsF3YpYE3oeMCYC6x6R6hrCI4oqHtV9paOlSZ21c_OypTy9fan504oYTjE3XywuR13a82R_XwVRidOf0oHO9uqWli6QvkExE_THqbhmD7GuqJ0w"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/Weff8wqn810hKciXuD4p8NmdoSsF3YpYE3oeMCYC6x6R6hrCI4oqHtV9paOlSZ21c_OypTy9fan504oYTjE3XywuR13a82R_XwVRidOf0oHO9uqWli6QvkExE_THqbhmD7GuqJ0w" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 8: Variables and their values of PowerShell command</figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/aiFzuPewyFn81hTNFPD1Ju8AxERkG31QjoB-S9UtZfwQoxAUpLUCa6P5XEzfOc7FnDRmoJC4Nz6GlwMxIKp1q1_sidQGHH7Tcb4NwzRhv4pQrRlFRuYCoOBfGVnxJkuMg930qMM0"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/aiFzuPewyFn81hTNFPD1Ju8AxERkG31QjoB-S9UtZfwQoxAUpLUCa6P5XEzfOc7FnDRmoJC4Nz6GlwMxIKp1q1_sidQGHH7Tcb4NwzRhv4pQrRlFRuYCoOBfGVnxJkuMg930qMM0" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 9: PowerShell command</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-defense-evasion">Defense Evasion</h2>



<p>In order to evade defense measures, the Revenge RAT malware contains &#8220;Server.txt&#8221;, which is an obfuscated PowerShell file containing several variables. These variables represent four malicious files that are created on the user&#8217;s computer.</p>



<p>The file starts its execution by checking if the folder it wants to create already exists. If it doesn&#8217;t, then it creates the folder.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/-kBuEWKm-glGsrfzPX6-SvA4HYkNQUjrFqTbl9oGNo7Hs18sZ1NPLy6WFpvftoHvdGh-IkHdnFfhTG7BWktOfYVkqd4HfzS3lG7JxGZ-vppJz1nyDQmRdvpmxvBORv1e0jeVmZO4"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/-kBuEWKm-glGsrfzPX6-SvA4HYkNQUjrFqTbl9oGNo7Hs18sZ1NPLy6WFpvftoHvdGh-IkHdnFfhTG7BWktOfYVkqd4HfzS3lG7JxGZ-vppJz1nyDQmRdvpmxvBORv1e0jeVmZO4" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 10: Folder path “C:\ProgramData\IICOZODNLSRPOHUBAJWZFK”:</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/KkxlB7-EbIjPmpdEqXUOY9rmuYB_xRx1HyDq-15qRJiHVARGDr2Kz2TvjwTBAwGHcOFRg9NKkeKHo5iow5tcPAOHp_qxckb4wSASxOeqdYElOOlxjmLqe7rnQ90fzPw8RuSllKUC"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/KkxlB7-EbIjPmpdEqXUOY9rmuYB_xRx1HyDq-15qRJiHVARGDr2Kz2TvjwTBAwGHcOFRg9NKkeKHo5iow5tcPAOHp_qxckb4wSASxOeqdYElOOlxjmLqe7rnQ90fzPw8RuSllKUC" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 11: 4 malicious files in the newly created folder</figcaption></figure>

<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/rNMJ51j-T8faZBoEPoyZWSwd0KAQhWh-2_t6za5uHkdJTnMFJ_U7JeyH5lhLkatzOVitdtkz5fG43Y72ri3LhpvijncAdLKfXN0lMv50iqSc_qbClroilz8vr0XLirRjbvVKKmr0"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/rNMJ51j-T8faZBoEPoyZWSwd0KAQhWh-2_t6za5uHkdJTnMFJ_U7JeyH5lhLkatzOVitdtkz5fG43Y72ri3LhpvijncAdLKfXN0lMv50iqSc_qbClroilz8vr0XLirRjbvVKKmr0" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 16: YHZYJGKGYAFXQEZFSRCPPY.ps1: Contains two obfuscated files that are stored inside 2 variables (H5[Lime.dll], H6[Client.exe]). <p>&nbsp;</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-c-c-server-connection">C&amp;C Server Connection</h3>



<p>As previously mentioned, the command executes the Revenge RAT malware. We managed to extract the executable file from the obfuscated PowerShell file to provide a better overview of the payload.</p>



<p>The screenshot below shows basic information of the payload executable:</p>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignleft"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/u1eNoyRGhL3nzNXiqYc0bogI4VYUKXWlELKT5jiHoQnda_79j4lycRG70lPJB5RUHpk5Lwz1ucu8VjcQTaPuv0hUBF0v5C7YRy4mqZeayHEkPuCLVzmiy0RuI70HfmnSS_r_X-sI"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh4.googleusercontent.com/u1eNoyRGhL3nzNXiqYc0bogI4VYUKXWlELKT5jiHoQnda_79j4lycRG70lPJB5RUHpk5Lwz1ucu8VjcQTaPuv0hUBF0v5C7YRy4mqZeayHEkPuCLVzmiy0RuI70HfmnSS_r_X-sI" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 19: Payload executable information</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>We can see that the code is actually compiled with .NET, meaning we can open the executable in DnSpy and view the code. Surprisingly, this malware&#8217;s code is readable and not obfuscated.</p>



<p>One of the functions present in the code is “Config”, which contains the configuration of the RAT:</p>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" width="977" height="789" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20977%20789'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" class="wp-image-16975" data-lazy-srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1.png.webp 977w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1-300x242.png.webp 300w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1-768x620.png.webp 768w" data-lazy-sizes="(max-width: 977px) 100vw, 977px" data-lazy-src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1.png.webp" /><noscript><img decoding="async" width="977" height="789" src="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1.png.webp" alt="" class="wp-image-16975" srcset="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1.png.webp 977w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1-300x242.png.webp 300w,  https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/21-1-768x620.png.webp 768w" sizes="(max-width: 977px) 100vw, 977px" /></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 20: Configuration of the RAT</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Revenge RAT malware Config Class</h3>



<p>Below are the members of the config class and their functionality:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>Host: C&amp;C Server</li>



<li>Port: C&amp;C Port</li>



<li>ID: Unique ID of the installed RAT</li>



<li>Key: Magic string used as a separator to split data on the packets</li>



<li>CurrentMutex: Mutex placed by the RAT on the system</li>



<li>Splitter: Magic string used as packet end string</li>



<li>Stopwatch(): Member function used to reset stopwatch</li>
</ul>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">From computer to C2 server</h3>



<p>The first packet sent from the user’s computer to the C2 server contains lots of sensitive data related to the user’s computer. The data collected using a custom class presents the code named “IdGenerator”. Below are some of the methods the class uses to retrieve sensitive data:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>GetHardDiskSerialNumber: Get hard disk serial number</li>



<li>GetIp: Get IP address</li>



<li>GetCamera: Get information about the camera</li>



<li>GetSystem: Get processor information</li>



<li>GetCpu: Get CPU information</li>



<li>GetAV: Get the antiviruses installed on the system</li>



<li>GetActiveWindow: Get active window or window of the application used by the user</li>
</ul>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/yORwItH4Dy2F9yJqTF9hfnWQTaABICZeOrV8aWe1DAnnZlQXV9VdkrwWR0bO8ea5qm2iptPdsuVsyWwMyfIfSW-OdOZrDFfP8ozWCQOPmdeXb-aRQfizKNlSfgT6LROFn0iaai1k"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/yORwItH4Dy2F9yJqTF9hfnWQTaABICZeOrV8aWe1DAnnZlQXV9VdkrwWR0bO8ea5qm2iptPdsuVsyWwMyfIfSW-OdOZrDFfP8ozWCQOPmdeXb-aRQfizKNlSfgT6LROFn0iaai1k" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 21: The first packet sent: Information<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>TVJfYWhtZWQ=<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>X0IwQTAzOUM1<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>169.254.102.77<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>REVTS1RPUC0zQUs1RTBVIC8gRmxhcmUtVk0=<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>No<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>TWljcm9zb2Z0IFdpbmRvd3MgMTAgUHJvIDY0<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>SW50ZWwoUikgQ29yZShUTSkgaTUtOTQwMEYgQ1BVIEAgMi45MEdIeg==<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>4294496256<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>V2luZG93cyBEZWZlbmRlcg==<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>Ti9B<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>2222<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>UHJvZ3JhbSBNYW5hZ2Vy<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>ZW4tVVM=<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">Revenge-RAT</span>False<span class="has-inline-color has-vivid-red-color">!@#%^NYAN#!@$</span></figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>This packet contains everything the RAT malware collected using the function we explained above. Now, when the malware establishes its connection, it can send commands that the user&#8217;s malware agent will handle.</p>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-c-c-commands-execution">C&amp;C Commands Execution</h2>



<p>One of the classes within the code is called “PacketHandler”; it contains a function called “Handler”. &#8220;Handler&#8221; is in charge of handling all received C&amp;C commands. The C2 server can send 5 commands to the user’s computer:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>PNC: Ping/Heartbeat command, which makes sure the connection is still alive.</li>



<li>P: Sends the name of the current working window on the user’s computer.</li>



<li>IE: Checks for installed plugin in the registry of the user’s computer under “HKCU\Software\”</li>



<li>LP: If the plugin in IE command isn’t found, the malware creates a new registry key under “HKCU\Software\” named after the RAT mutex (base64 encoded) with a subkey, named after the value inside of it (MD5 hashed) and the value of the subkey. This becomes a dll file sent on this command in order to maintain persistence and for further execution.</li>
</ul>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/tG4gl2S5CCbXT7hbq75cvmT-GWJkR0zOI9Sl5_wEW-QTDrLEfBM0wWwuYhaoWKvs_OFznRW0EGPdKp1FL6farMSphm23XIkvjZi3-NsGzmKMmnbLVa9qbHAdTUA9EfMYWCM6OybT"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/tG4gl2S5CCbXT7hbq75cvmT-GWJkR0zOI9Sl5_wEW-QTDrLEfBM0wWwuYhaoWKvs_OFznRW0EGPdKp1FL6farMSphm23XIkvjZi3-NsGzmKMmnbLVa9qbHAdTUA9EfMYWCM6OybT" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 22: &#8220;Handler&#8221; function</figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%200%200'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="" data-lazy-src="https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/UaIWdLduLhG-v9zHaMFDHFljWAd6YrhPA7gSOJyehJF88aaLOEpfTy4I9HF6OhGb3q4c3Lt3QPeOn0pGXd3JXXY8k3Juz-U5iuAEDYvZLg4OJ4f3ytmw7fVOtZ-NXMb4Va8TqK9D"/><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://lh6.googleusercontent.com/UaIWdLduLhG-v9zHaMFDHFljWAd6YrhPA7gSOJyehJF88aaLOEpfTy4I9HF6OhGb3q4c3Lt3QPeOn0pGXd3JXXY8k3Juz-U5iuAEDYvZLg4OJ4f3ytmw7fVOtZ-NXMb4Va8TqK9D" alt=""/></noscript><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 23: U<span style="color: initial;">NV: Uninstalls and restarts the RAT; it also creates a script saved in the temporary folder on the user’s computer that, once it’s run by the malware,&nbsp;deletes the original “jsc.exe” file.</span></figcaption></figure>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-recommendations">Recommendations</h2>



<p>Revenge RAT malware causes a massive problems when deployed on a highly valued employee&#8217;s computer. Most likely delivered via a <a href="https://perception-point.io/guides/phishing/how-to-prevent-phishing-attacks/">phishing email</a>, it targets the individual person. It also requires the user’s attention to run the macros in order to initiate the attack.</p>



<p>To avoid falling victim to this type of attack,&nbsp;we recommend the following steps to mitigate your organization’s risk:</p>



<p>1.&nbsp; Educate your employees on the need for email security and the risk of opening suspicious emails and attachments.</p>



<p>2.&nbsp; Run email security drills every few months, to ensure that employees know what to look for in a suspicious email.&nbsp;</p>



<p>3.&nbsp; Create a process for employees to follow when they receive a suspicious email or link.</p>



<p>4.&nbsp; Disable macros in Microsoft Office applications.</p>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>For more information about how to protect your organization against malware attacks delivered via phishing emails, read our <a href="https://perception-point.io/wp-content/uploads/Advanced-Email-Security.pdf">Advanced Email Security guide</a> today.</p>



<p>&nbsp;</p>



<p>Here&#8217;s some related content you may enjoy: <a href="https://perception-point.io/guides/malware/how-prevent-malware-attacks/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">How to Prevent Malware Attacks</a></p>

    function handleFormSubmit(ele) {
        var submitButton = ele.querySelector('input[type=submit]');
        var spinner = document.createElement('span');
        spinner.setAttribute('class', 'loader');
        submitButton.setAttribute('disabled', true);
        submitButton.style.cursor = 'wait';
        submitButton.parentNode.appendChild(spinner);
        return true;
    }

    function resetSubmitButton(e) {
        var submitButtons = e.target.form.getElementsByClassName('submit-button');
        for (var i = 0; i < submitButtons.length; i++) {
            submitButtons[i].disabled = false;
        }
    }

    function addChangeHandler(elements) {
        for (var i = 0; i < elements.length; i++) {
            elements[i].addEventListener('change', resetSubmitButton);
        }
    }

<script type="rocketlazyloadscript">
    document.onload = handleDocumentLoad('form11580', '3049749');

    function handleDocumentLoad(b, a) {
        window.getElqFormSubmissionToken(b, a);
        window.processLastFormField()
    }

    function getElqFormSubmissionToken(g, c) {
        var e = new XMLHttpRequest();
        var b = document.getElementById(g);
        if (b && b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken")) {
            var f = b.action;
            var a = window.getHostName(f);
            a = "https://" + a + "/e/formsubmittoken?elqSiteID=" + c;
            if (a) {
                e.onreadystatechange = function () {
                    if (e.readyState === 4) {
                        if (e.status === 200) {
                            b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken").value = e.responseText
                        } else {
                            b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken").value = ""
                        }
                    }
                };
                e.open("GET", a, true);
                e.send()
            } else {
                b.elements.namedItem("elqFormSubmissionToken").value = ""
            }
        }
    }

    function getHostName(b) {
        if (typeof window.URL === "function") {
            return new window.URL(b).hostname
        } else {
            var a = b.match(/:\/\/(www[0-9]?\.)?(.[^\/:]+)/i);
            if (a !== null && a.length > 2 && typeof a[2] === "string" && a[2].length > 0) {
                return a[2]
            } else {
                return null
            }
        }
    }

    function processLastFormField() {
        var form = document.getElementById("form11580");
        var lastFormField = form.querySelector("#elq-FormLastRow");
        lastFormField.style.display = "none";
    }

				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
									In recent months, threat actors have discovered a troubling new method to exploit URL rewriting, a feature intended to protect users by replacing links in emails. By manipulating these rewritten URLs, attackers mask highly evasive phishing links behind trusted domains of security vendors, effectively bypassing detection. This abuse has led to a critical surge in advanced phishing attacks that leverage the very tools developed to prevent them.								</div>

					const lazyloadBackgrounds = document.querySelectorAll( `.e-con.e-parent:not(.e-lazyloaded)` );
					const lazyloadBackgroundObserver = new IntersectionObserver( ( entries ) => {
						entries.forEach( ( entry ) => {
							if ( entry.isIntersecting ) {
								let lazyloadBackground = entry.target;
								if( lazyloadBackground ) {
									lazyloadBackground.classList.add( 'e-lazyloaded' );
								}
								lazyloadBackgroundObserver.unobserve( entry.target );
							}
						});
					}, { rootMargin: '200px 0px 200px 0px' } );
					lazyloadBackgrounds.forEach( ( lazyloadBackground ) => {
						lazyloadBackgroundObserver.observe( lazyloadBackground );
					} );
				};

					'DOMContentLoaded',
					'elementor/lazyload/observe',
				];
